About this workbook 

Welcome to this Infor Education course! We hope you will find this learning experience enjoyable and 
instructive. This Training Workbook is designed to support the following forms of learning: 

•  Classroom instructor-led training 
•  Virtual instructor-led training 
•  Self-directed learning 

This Training Workbook is not intended for use as a product user guide. 

Activity data 

You will be asked to complete some practice exercises during this course. Step-by-step instructions are 
provided in this guide to assist you with completing the exercises. Where necessary, data columns are 
included for your reference. 

Your instructor will provide more information on systems used in class, including server addresses, login 
IDs, and passwords. 

Self-directed learning 

If you are taking this course as self-directed learning, there may be instructor-recorded presentations 
and/or simulations available to assist you.  

If instructor-recorded presentations are available, a hyperlink to the recording will be included on the first 
page of each corresponding Lesson. 

If simulations are available, the demos and exercises throughout this Training Workbook will include 
hyperlinks that allow you to view and/or practice the execution of the demo or exercise in a simulated 
training environment. 

Learning Libraries 

Learning Libraries in Infor Campus include learning materials that are available to you online, anytime, 
anywhere. These materials can supplement instructor-led training, providing you with additional learning 
resources to support your day-to-day business tasks and activities.  

Please note that if you accessed this Training Workbook directly via a Learning Library, you will not have 
access to the Infor Education Training Environment that is provided with all instructor-led and most self-
directed learning course versions, as referenced above. Therefore, you will not be able to practice the 
exercises in the specific Training Environment for which the exercises in this Training Workbook were 
written.

10   About this workbook 

© 2017 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Course overview 

Estimated time 

.25 hours 

Learning objectives 

Upon completion of this course, you should be able to: 

Identify the forms used for the Application Event System.  

•  Describe the Application Event System (AES). 
•  Describe the functionality of events and event handlers. 
•  Define framework events. 
• 
•  Define the purpose of an event global constant. 
•  Describe key concepts for creating custom events and handlers.   
•  Define the New Workflow Wizard. 
•  Discuss how event messages work with the Inbox form. 
• 
Identify the forms used to track event system status.  
•  Explain how to set up an event that uses a prompt to request approval. 
•  Explain how to set up an event to add information to a record. 
•  Explain how to set up an event to request multiple levels of approval. 
•  Explain how to generate an event from within a form. 
•  List the steps for setting up external email. 
•  Match voting rules to their definitions. 

Topics 

•  Course description and agenda 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

11 

© 2017 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Course description and agenda 

This course covers the infrastructure and functionality of the Application Event System. It includes hands-
on practice designing and setting up typical system events such as email notifications, approval requests, 
and voting. This training is for versions: 10.18.00 and all previous versions. Previous course name: Infor 
Mongoose Guide to the Application Event System. 

Prerequisite courses 

•  User Interface Part 1: Finding and working with forms 
•  User Interface Part 2: Finding and displaying records 
•  User Interface Part 3: Adding, editing, and deleting records 
•  User Interface Part 4: Additional record management 
•  Optional: CloudSuite Foundation 101 

Course duration 

16 hours 

Prerequisite knowledge 
To optimize your learning experience, Infor recommends that you have the following knowledge prior to 
attending this course: 

•  Basic knowledge of database query syntax  

Audience 

•  Customer User  
•  Pre-Sales Consultant  
•  Business Consultant  
•  Technical Consultant  
•  Support 
•  System Administrator  

System requirements 

• 

Infor CloudSuite Training Environment

User interface 

Standard user interface elements include the navigation pane menus and the main toolbar. 

Navigation pane menus and main toolbar 

Navigation pane menus 

You can browse all available folders through the navigation pane menus and submenus. Click the down 
arrow for each of the navigation pane menus, this allows you to explore the submenus contained 
beneath. 

Navigation pane menus and submenus 

For information on how to pin submenus to the navigation pane, please refer to the Creating Form 
Personalizations workbook or ILT. For example, if you know that you will be using the Customer Service 
submenus frequently, you can display the menus at the top level, by pinning them to the navigation pane. 

Main toolbar 

The main toolbar contains icons that allow you to quickly complete commonly used tasks, such as saving 
data or closing a form. You can position your mouse pointer over an icon on the toolbar to reveal its 
description and functionality.  

To view a list of all toolbar icons and for steps on how to add/remove icons from the default toolbar, See 
Appendix B. Note: Availability to make hiding buttons visible is not available for Web users. 

Keyboard shortcuts 

Some navigation pane submenus display available keyboard shortcuts that can be used to save multiple 
steps when completing certain tasks.

Field types 

The following tables display the field types available with a brief description: 

Field type 

Description 

Required, system-generated, and read-only fields

About the Application Event System 

The Application Event System is a powerful and flexible set of tools designed to help extend and 
customize any business process in CloudSuite. You can use these tools to define and monitor events that 
take can place anywhere in the system, on any form.   

Think of an event occurrence as a “package” that contains three parts: a condition, a set of rules, and a 
list of actions. The rules will begin to execute the actions whenever the condition becomes true.  

Let’s say you want to be alerted when a new product is added to the system. You can use the Application 
Event System to write simple rules to be notified automatically. The trigger condition becomes true when 
any system user adds a new item record. When that condition is met, your rules define what actions the 
system should take, in this case sending you a message about the new product.  

The Application Event System can support highly complex conditions too. It can support event actions 
that can update any data in the application, automate any process provided by the application, prompt a 
user or group of users to approve a change, integrate to an external application, or simply notify users of 
a change.  

Anyone can use the Application Event System because it allows you to define the rules without writing 
any code. In this course, we will provide a good grounding in the system basics first. Later, we will guide 
you step-by-step through a range of typical scenarios that you can modify and use in your own 
organization.  

24   Lesson 1: Introduction to the Application Event System 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
Basic components 

In its most basic form, the Application Event System consists of three interrelated components: events, 
event handlers, and event actions. The diagram below illustrates the relationship between these 
components. 

Event 

  Event  
  handler  
  “A” 

Event action 1 

Event action 2 

  Event  
  handler  
  “B” 

Event action 1 

Event action 2 

Event action 2 

  Event  
  handler  
  “C” 

Event action 1 

Event action 2 

Events, event handlers, and event actions 

Let’s explore each component separately.  

Events 

Events are uniquely named incidents that can occur during the use of an application. Events can have 
multiple triggers and can be generated by user actions, conditions in a database, other events, or other 
situations. 

Event handlers 

Event handlers are associated with one event and one or more event actions. When an event occurs, an 
event handler evaluates the event and determines what actions, if any, to initiate. Essentially, an event 
handler is a set of instructions that describes how the system should respond to a particular event. An 
event handler consists of data that specifies: 

•  The event to which it is associated.  
•  Any conditions, situations, or attributes that determine when and why it should execute. 
•  One or more event actions to take place when it executes. 

An event can have multiple handlers but each handler can be associated with only one event. 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

25 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Event actions 

Event actions consist of data that specifies the individual tasks or bits of work that are performed by the 
event handler. Each event handler must have at least one event action and can have multiple actions. 

Now let’s look at an example. 

Example ► Order approval 

Let’s say you want the system to automatically notify you whenever someone adds a customer order to 
the system and to request your approval if the order is $1000 or more.  

Here are the components as they apply to this example: 

•  The event gets triggered whenever someone creates a new customer order. In this example, we 

have named the event OrderNotification.  

•  The event handler is set up to execute when the event occurs and any situational conditions 

have been met. 

•  The event actions that you want to take place are to notify you that an order has been placed 

and to request your approval for orders of $1000 or more. 

Conceptually, the event, event handlers, and event actions for this example could be illustrated as shown 
in the following diagram.  

Event: 
OrderNotification 

  Event  
  handler 

Event action 1: 
The system checks the amount of the 
order: 

• If less than $1000, go to event action 

3 

Event action 2: 
The system sends a message requesting 
manager approval, then waits for approval 
before continuing.  

Event action 3: 
The system sends a message to the 
manager’s Inbox notifying him or her that 
the transaction is complete.   

Conceptual diagram for new customer order approval 

Only a single event handler is needed for this example, but that event handler requires several actions to 
complete all the requirements.  

26   Lesson 1: Introduction to the Application Event System 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
  
•  The first action is to check the amount of the order and determine, based on the amount, what 
additional actions are required. If the order is $1000 or more, then it must direct the flow to 
another action that requests a manager’s approval. If the order is less than $1000, then it directs 
the flow to yet another action that simply sends out a notification that the order has been placed. 

•  The second action is used only if the order is $1000 or more. If this action is used, the system 

generates a prompt message to the manager, requesting approval for the order. The system then 
waits until the manager responds. If the manager approves the order, the system then proceeds 
to the next action.  

•  The third action is used regardless of the amount of the order. This action sends out a notification 

to let the manager know that the order has been completed. 

End of example 

In the example, what happens if the manager does not approve the order? Please consider 
your response before reading the answer.  

Answer: If the manager does not approve the order, the event handler fails and no further 
action is taken. The example was specifically kept simple, so it does not provide for the case 
where the order is not approved. Please note that as a good general practice, an event 
handler should address all possibilities.

Advantages 

The Application Event System offers several significant advantages over hard-coded systems, by helping 
you to: 

•  Reduce or eliminate the need for procedural code. 
•  Prevent situations where event handlers could be inadvertently changed. 
•  Provide an easier way to gain control of the application flow. 

Let’s review each in more detail.  

Reduce or eliminate the need for procedural code 

The Application Event System provides a flexible way to implement event functionality, often without 
having to modify any procedural code. Metadata is used to define event handlers and actions which 
eliminates (or greatly reduces) the need for procedural code.   

The AES infrastructure allows both the framework and application code to generate events, and for 
developers and system administrators to create handlers for those events. Event handlers can augment 
or replace the default framework or application behavior associated with the event. You can design event 
handlers so that maximal work can be performed without requiring new procedural code. 

In this context, the following definitions apply: 

•  Procedural code refers to compiled code.   
•  Metadata refers to the practice of using uncompiled code and information about data 

formats that are interpreted during run time. 

Prevent situations where event handlers could be inadvertently changed  

System upgrades will not change or overwrite any of the event functionality that you have created with 
AES. Because event handlers are defined using metadata, any potential upgrade problems are 
prevented.  

Additionally, there are no collision problems if multiple developers have created event handlers for the 
same event. Events and event handlers are tagged at the time of their creation with a special identifier 
(Access As) that prevents them from being modified or deleted by other development organizations. 
Among other things, this prevents your custom events and handlers from being overwritten when Infor or 
another developer issues updates to their events and handlers. 

Provide an easier way to gain control of the application flow  

You can use your event handlers with events created by others to gain control of the application flow, 
augmenting it and even altering default system behaviors. This allows you to take appropriate actions, 
without being forced to call into the application using application program interfaces (APIs).   

28   Lesson 1: Introduction to the Application Event System 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Examples 

The following examples show how to use the Application Event System to address a variety of common 
business needs. Later in this course, you will define event handlers and actions for similar scenarios.  

Example 1 ► Send notification when a new record is added 

Currently, you must rely on everyone who adds customers, trusting that they will remember to send an 
email notifying the sales manager of each new customer.  

The Application Event System can fully automate compliance of the sales manager’s request. You can 
set up an event that is generated whenever anyone adds a new customer into the system, and use event 
handlers and actions to automatically generate a notification that is sent directly to the sales manager. 

Example 2 ► Get approval for a credit limit change 

Your company requires that any change to a customer’s credit limit be approved by a designated credit 
manager. Currently, you rely on customer representatives to contact that credit manager whenever a 
change to a credit limit is requested. 

The Application Event System can be set up to automatically send the credit manager a message 
requesting approval of the change. To speed up the process, you also send an email to the credit 
manager with links the manager can click to approve or deny the request. When the credit manager 
responds to the message and approves the request, the system can then automatically change the credit 
limit amount. 

Example 3 ► Complex approval of a purchase order status change 

Suppose your company has a multi-tiered business approval process in place that functions as follows: 

•  Approval is requested from a purchasing manager when the status of any purchase order (PO) is 

• 

• 

• 

changed to an “ordered” status. 
If approved, and if the request and the total cost of the PO exceeds a certain amount, majority 
approval is required from a group of mid-level managers on whether the order should be sent to 
the vendor. 
If those managers also approve and the total cost of the PO exceeds another, higher amount, 
then a request is sent requiring unanimous approval from a group of top-level directors. 
If the request is approved at all the required levels for the total cost of the PO, then the 
transaction is approved and completed. 

The process described in this example could all be taken care of manually. However, it would involve 
actions by many employees and the risk of something getting missed would increase with each tier of the 
approval process. 

With the Application Event System, you could use events and handlers to automate this entire process, 
evaluating the PO at each step of the way, requesting only the necessary approvals, and completing the 
transaction. 

Example 4 ► Automatically ship a customer order 

Suppose you have a system that, by default, requires someone to manually set the system to ship a 
customer order line when the status of the line is set to “filled.” This can cause delays in orders, if/when 
the responsible employee does not set the order to ship in a timely manner. 

You can use the Application Event System to “cut out the middle-man” and automatically set the order to 
ship as soon as the status is set to “filled.” 

End of examples

Predefined workflow event handlers 

The Application Event System is extremely powerful and flexible. However, for some non-developer 
users, the system can be somewhat overwhelming at first.  

To help you with the learning curve, Infor provides a set of predefined workflow event handlers. You can 
access the full set from the Workflow Event Handler Activation form.   

These handlers are almost ready-to-use. In most cases, all you need to do is define the user names or 
email addresses of the people who should be notified when a certain event happens, and activate the 
handler. 

Here are some examples of the more than fifty predefined handlers:  

•  Alert on shipping order 
•  Alert users of a new vendor document 
•  Alert when order shipment is late 
•  Alert when account’s actual > budget 
•  Approve customer credit limit change 
•  Approve PO line amount 
•  Notify customer created 
•  Notify invoice due date changed 
•  Prospect interaction follow-up alert 
•  Send portal question/comment email 

For example, if a customer's credit limit is changed, the "Approve customer credit limit change" workflow 
event handler can automatically send an email to the Accounts Receivable manager and request 
approval of the change. To set this up, just specify the manager's email address and activate the handler 
on the Workflow Event Handler Activation form.

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18)

How events are handled 

When the event is generated, it causes the event handlers to evaluate the conditions and fire any 
associated event actions that are called for by those conditions.  

Essentially, when an event is generated, it requires an event handler to do something in response. 
Otherwise, the event serves no practical purpose. In other words, if there is no handler for an event, the 
event actually does nothing when it is generated.  

Some events, such as framework events, intentionally do not have an event handler. 

34   Lesson 2: Understanding how the system works 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
Framework events 

Framework events (or core events) are events predefined in the system that have been designed to be 
generated only about certain “framework” occurrences. These events—which include system actions like 
adding a record, modifying a record, deleting a record, and so on—cannot be generated 
programmatically. That is, you cannot define framework events; they simply get generated every time the 
system does something.  

Now, what this means is that events are constantly being generated in CloudSuite applications. Most of 
the time, you are not aware of them and the system does nothing in response to them. That is because, if 
no event handler has been defined to respond to an event, no response is generated. The event occurs, 
and that is that.  

We can, however, define event handlers to respond to these framework events, and that is a large part of 
the operations that occur in the system.  

Framework events were created as a starting point for developers to use with custom 
event handlers. 

Infor includes a set of framework events as part of the system. These events deliberately do not include 
handlers. Examples of framework events are:  

• 
• 
• 

IdoOnItemInsert 
IdoOnItemUpdate 
IdoPostItemDelete 

A list of all framework events is located in Appendix A. 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18)

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Controlling the sequence of event handlers and 
event actions 

Sequence number settings are used to control the order in which event handlers and their actions will 
execute.  

Event handler sequence numbers 

Each event handler has a handler sequence number assigned by the system. Handlers execute in the 
order of their numeric sequence numbers unless you make modifications. 

There are two methods for modifying the default handler sequence:  

•  Using the Keep With and Chronology fields on the Event Handlers form 
•  Using the Event Handler Sequence form  

This topic is covered in more detail in Lesson 4 of this Training Workbook under the topic 
“Event handling and order.” 

Event action sequence numbers 

The event actions associated with each event handler also have their own action sequence numbers. 
These actions will always execute in numeric order, unless at least one of the following is true: 

•  You use the Initial Action option to designate that a particular action should execute first.  

Note: This option is found on the Event Handlers form. 

•  You use certain action types to modify the flow.

Restricting which handlers run 

There might be times when you need to disable the event handlers created by one or more development 
organizations, including yours, at least temporarily. This would occur typically when you are 
troubleshooting problems with the event system. The system offers several ways to disable event 
handlers. 

Using the Event Handler form 

When you want to disable an individual handler with your Access As value, do both of the following:  

•  Open the Event Handler form and for each event handler that you do not want to include in 

testing or debugging. 

•  Clear the Active check box. 

When you are finished testing, and want to enable the handlers, return to the Event Handler form and 
select the Active check box for each handler that you previously disabled.  

Include or exclude using the Session Access As form 

Access As identifies who owns and can modify an event or event handler in the system. 

When you want to enable or disable all the event handlers created by a particular developing organization 
all at once, you can use the Session Access As form. To accomplish this, specify which Access As 
identifiers should have their event handlers execute (included) and which should be ignored (excluded).

Synchronicity 

Synchronous events 

A synchronous event is expected to execute and run through to completion in the generating thread 
(unless the event is designed to suspend) before the thread continues. Characteristics of a synchronous 
event are:  

•  The event runs in the same thread that generated it.  
•  The event’s synchronous event handlers will execute in sequence.  
•  The event handlers will block the generating thread until they have all been executed or until a 

handler exits with a failure status. 

Asynchronous events 

An asynchronous event runs in a different thread than the one that posted it, usually on a utility server. In 
this case, the event does not block the generating thread, running independently of it. As soon as an 
acknowledgment is received that the event was successfully queued, the thread that generated the event 
continues. 

Generating synchronous or asynchronous events 

The following table shows how an event is synchronous or asynchronous depending on the choices you 
make when generating the event. 

If you generate an event … 

Using a framework (core) event 

Using a form event handler with a Generate Application Event response type AND 
the Synchronous check box is selected 

Then it is… 

Synchronous 

Synchronous 

Using a form event handler with a Generate Application Event response type AND 
the Synchronous check box is cleared 

Asynchronous 

Calling the FireApplicationEvent() .NET method AND the Synchronous parameter 
set to True 

Synchronous  

Calling the FireApplicationEvent() .NET method AND the Synchronous parameter 
set to False 

Asynchronous 

Calling this stored procedure:  

dbo.FireEventSp 

Calling this stored procedure:  

dbo.PostEventSp 

Using an event action of the type Generate Event AND the parameter of 
Synchronous is set to True  

Using an event action of the type Generate Event AND the parameter of 
Synchronous is set to False 

Synchronous 

Asynchronous 

Synchronous 

Asynchronous 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

39 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Synchronous and asynchronous event handlers 

A synchronous event handler is one that must complete before the system continues to the next event 
handler in a sequence. All synchronous handlers in the sequence must complete successfully for the 
entire event to be handled successfully. 

There is an exception to this rule. If one event handler fails for a reason other than for an illegal operation, 
and the system is set to ignore failures for that handler, the system can continue to the next synchronous 
event handler. Otherwise, the system returns a failure error, and no more event handlers in the sequence 
will execute. 

By contrast, an asynchronous event handler runs independently of other event handlers. 

Event handlers can be designated as synchronous or asynchronous at the time they are 
created, selecting (or clearing) the Synchronous check box on the Event Handlers form. 

Any event can execute an asynchronous event handler; that is, an event handler for which the 
Synchronous check box is cleared. The system sends the asynchronous event handler to the event 
handler queue, a process called queueing. The system does this by means of the PostEventHandlerSp 
stored procedure, to which it passes the configuration name from the event state.  

Two outcomes can occur: 

• 

• 

If queueing was successful, the event's thread continues to the next event handler or, if no 
subsequent handlers are defined, completes the event. 
If queueing was unsuccessful, the event stops with a failure condition. 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

40   Lesson 2: Understanding how the system works 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Suspension 

Suspension is when a requested operation is sent to the event system for completion later. In simpler 
terms, a suspended operation is one that is paused and waiting for something to occur before it can 
continue. A common reason for suspending an operation is when the system sends a message that 
requires a response; until the response is received, the system pauses while it waits for a response—and 
it remains in suspension until it receives a response.   

You can define an event handler to suspend by selecting the Suspend option and defining an adjourning 
action.  

An adjourning action, such as receiving a response to a request, causes the system to stop 
the suspension and continue to the next action.  

Suspension is possible only with certain framework events. Currently, there are only three events that can 
be suspended: 

• 
• 
• 

IdoOnItemInsert 
IdoOnItemUpdate 
IdoOnItemDelete 

You cannot create custom events that can be suspended. 

For suspension to occur, the following must be true: 

•  The event must be one of the three framework events that can be suspended. 
•  You must select the Suspend check box for at least one handler (on the Event Handler form) 

that applies to the generated event’s object and initiator. 

When both above conditions are met, the system passes control of the requested update 
(insertion/update/deletion) to the event system. The event system then uses a step-by-step process to try 
to make sure that all the event handlers can execute successfully before committing the system (and the 
data) to execution of the event. 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

41 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Suspend-validating mode

outlined in the chart below. 

Step

1 

2 

3 

4 

5 

Begins a database 
transaction 

Performs an update to the 
database, to validate 
pertinent data against any 
SQL constraints or 
triggers 

Validates the data by 
running all effective event 
handlers that are 
synchronous and not 
suspended in the current 
transaction 

N/A 

N/A 

This step involves both application and event system 
changes. 

At the beginning of this process, in a separate event 
transaction, the system copies the event handler to a new 
revision, if necessary. 

•  The reason for this is that both the suspend-validating

back. So, the current event handler revision must be 
available, in case someone edits the handlers between the

When an event is not suspended 

When an event that can be suspended is not suspended (that is, no event handler that applies to that 
event’s object and initiator has the Suspend check box selected), the system executes the event using 
the steps outlined in the chart that follows.  

Step 

The system … 

1 

2 

3 

Performs a database update 

Executes all effective event handlers, returning either success or failure 

Handles failures as errors and exits the event 

Each insertion/update/deletion request in the process—which could be from an external XML request or 
from a Save action in WinStudio—participates in the overall transaction. In this case, all complete or 
nothing completes. So, each handler chain (the same chain executes for each insertion/ update/deletion 
in the group) also participates in that overall transaction.  

A failure of any handler chain results in the entire transaction being rolled back. 

Payload 

An event that can be suspended carries a payload. This payload represents the requested change (that 
is, insertion, update, or deletion) and can be accessed and modified by event handlers. For example, if an 
approval request is sent, the response contains the payload—yes or no.  

The value of each named property that forms part of the requested change is passed into and out of the 
event system as a parameter. The parameter uses the naming convention “Row.Property," where 
Property is the name of the IDO property. Any modified property values are accompanied by another 
parameter using the naming convention "Row.Property.Modified" and having value of “1.” 

During notify and prompt actions, these parameters are: 

•  Temporarily converted to event variables, which overrides any value that might have been set in 

preceding actions or specified on the Event Variable Groups form. 

•  Available for display (and update, for prompts) on the Variables tab of the Inbox form (and for 

display at the end of an external email prompt). 

•  Converted back to parameters for subsequent actions. 

During other actions such as Set Values, these parameters can be modified to affect the result of the 
change when it is applied to the database. This can be done by using this syntax: 

•  SETPROPVALUES("Property"=expression) – If the new value is different than the original 

framework event parameter received by the event, the PROPERTYMODIFIED(PropertyName) 
framework event parameter is also set to TRUE automatically 

•  SETPARMVALUES(Row.Property=expression, Row.Property.Modified="1") – Note that you 

should also indicate to the framework that the property is now modified 

44   Lesson 2: Understanding how the system works 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Adjournment and resumption 

Some chains of event actions might need to be temporarily stopped while waiting for some condition to be 
met. A temporary halt in executing the event handler chain is called adjournment.  

Example  Adjournment for manager approval 

Company XYZ requires a manager’s approval of a customer order before it can be entered into the 
system. They have set up the event handlers for adding a new customer order so that the system 
temporarily halts the execution of the event handler chain until the condition is met; that is, until the 
manager responds with an approval (or disapproval). The system sends a message requesting approval 
to the manager, and when a response is received, will resume executing the event handler chain.  

End of example 

Certain event actions, called adjourning actions, must wait for an external stimulus before the event 
handler can proceed with the next action. In the example given, the adjourning action was the message 
sent to the manager asking for approval.  

An event handler containing such an adjourning action must be either: 

•  Asynchronous  

or 

•  Part of a framework event that can be suspended and is marked to suspend  

When execution reaches an adjourning action, the event handler state is set to retest or time out at a 
specified time. At this point, the event becomes an adjourned event. 

The event service then processes it at the next opportunity after the Time Out and/or Retest time. This is 
called resumption.

_________________________________________________________________________

Success, failures, and retries 

Each event handler can exit with a status of Success or Failure. When an event handler fails, if it is not 
set to ignore failures, and if it has not attempted an illegal operation, the system can retry the event 
handler’s actions. 

An event handler exits with a  
status of Success when

An event handler exits with a  
status of Failure when

It reaches an event action of 
the type Finish OR

It reaches an event action of 
the type Fail  OR

It completes all event actions 
successfully without reaching an 
event action of type Fail

It attempts an illegal operation           

OR

It experiences an unexpected failure 
(of an event action or something 
launched by an event action)

Causes of an event handler’s success or failure 

When a failure occurs 

Here are the things that take place when a failure occurs: 

•  The Event Handler Status form’s Result field displays an appropriate error message, which 

includes an error message passed from a failing stored procedure in a message type parameter 
(@Infobar) where applicable. 

•  The current event action state's Times Failed counter is incremented. 
•  This value displays on the Actions tab of the Event Handler Status form. 
•  The event handler state's Last Activity Date is set to the date and time of the failure. 
•  This value displays on the Handlers tab of the Event Handler Status form. 
•  Any event variables are maintained at their current values for inspection on the Variables tab of 

the Event Handler Status form. 

Ignoring failures 

By default, if any synchronous event handler reports a failure, the system skips any remaining event 
handlers for that event and exits with a status of Failure. 

You can override this default behavior by using the Ignore Failure option for the event handler (on the 
Event Handlers form). If you select this option, the event handler is always treated as successful unless 
it deliberately attempts an illegal operation. The Status field on the Event Handler Status form, however, 
still shows a status of Failure, even though the Any Handlers Failed check box remains cleared. 

The table that follows shows what conditions can result in failures for various action types and whether 
the failure can be ignored.

Transactions 

A transactional event is an event or set of event handlers in which all must complete successfully before 
any data is committed in the system. If any handler fails, then the system rolls back (reverts) all data to its 
initial state. 

If the transactional event contains any adjourning event actions, the system modifies this behavior 
somewhat: Any actions taken up to the first adjournment, between each resumption of an adjournment 
and the beginning of the next adjournment, and after the last resumption, are each committed separately. 

The system treats any asynchronous event handlers as if they belong to an asynchronously generated 
non-transactional event. In other words, because they are outside the synchronous flow, these event 
handlers are not treated as part of the transaction. 

Transactions with synchronous events 

An event generated synchronously (without the Transactional check box selected on the Event 
Handlers form) does one of the following: 

•  Runs in the current transaction state of the firing thread (if no lower-level transactioning is 

specified) 

•  Handles transactions at the event handler or event action level, as explained in these points: 

Level 

Database tier 

Note: This occurs 
naturally with no special 
syntax. 

Explanation 

•  When FireEventSp is called within an SQL transaction, any changes 
to the non-event system portions of the database (using Audit, Call 
Database Method, and Run Background Task event actions) 
become a part of that transaction, which can be rolled back after 
FireEventSp returns. 

•  When FireEventSp is called outside an SQL transaction, each event 
action that might change non-event system portions of the database 
is wrapped in its own transaction, which is rolled back in case of 
failure, except when the containing event handler is marked 
Transactional. Changes to event system state data are performed 
on a separate connection, so they survive any rollback of the 
encompassing or wrapping transactions. 

Middle tier 

•  To include the entire event in a single transaction, call the 

Note: Any execution 
methods for database-
related event actions must 
enlist in an existing 
transaction or create a 
new one. 

FireApplicationEvent method from a hand-coded IDO method that is 
marked Transactional in the IDO metadata. In this case, the 
execution methods for the database-related event action enlist in 
the transaction created by the IDO Runtime for that transactional 
IDO method. 

•  When the FireApplicationEvent method is called from a hand-coded 
IDO method that is not marked Transactional and whose caller is 
not in a transaction state, each event action execution method 
creates a new transaction, which is rolled back in case of failure, 
except when the containing event handler is marked Transactional. 
Changes to event system state data are performed in a separate

MGCore.Events.FireEvent IDO method. 

A failure of a synchronous event returns an error condition to the firing thread. For framework (core) 
events, this causes the entire current transaction to be rolled back. For application events, whatever 
generated the event (stored procedure/trigger, .NET method, or VB.NET script) is responsible for trapping 
the error condition, accomplishing a rollback of the current transaction, and throwing execution to an 
appropriate recovery point in or out of that code. 

Event handlers marked transactional 

An event handler marked “Transactional” is wrapped in: 

•  A new SQL transaction when executed in the database tier. 
•  A new transaction when executed from the middle tier, if no encompassing transaction is active at 

the trigger point of the outermost enclosing event. 

This event handler cannot contain any adjourning event action types (for example, Prompt, Wait, or 
Sleep), but it can be marked Asynchronous itself. If this event handler ends in a failure, the wrapping 
transaction is rolled back (assuming it was created). 

Event handler not marked transactional 

An event generated asynchronously without the Transactional setting does not run in a transaction state. 
Event handlers that are not marked Transactional, for a synchronous event generated from a middle-tier 
non-transactional custom IDO method, are handled this way: 

•  An event can be generated asynchronously with a Transactional setting that indicates that a new 
transaction is begun and the entire database-related effects of its synchronous event handlers 
are committed if they all succeed or rolled back if any fail. 

•  Any asynchronous event handlers are treated as if they belong to an asynchronously generated 

non-transactional event. 

Rolling back transactions 

These conditions cause the system to set the Transactional setting on a new event state row: 

•  FireEventSp or FireApplicationEvent() detects an enclosing transaction 
•  FireEventSp, FireApplicationEvent(), PostEventSp, or PostEvent() receives a Transactional 

setting 

This event state setting signals the system administrator that when this event state is finished and not 
rolled back, everything happened that was expected to happen. 

50   Lesson 2: Understanding how the system works 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
In a synchronous multi-event situation, to encode the proper information in the Rolled Back setting, an 
enclosing transaction that contains one or more FireEventSp or FireApplicationEvent() calls must follow 
the certain steps.  

The following is a high-level overview of how to encode the proper information in the Rolled Back setting: 

Step 

How to encode information in the Rolled Back setting 

1 

Call EventBeginTransactionSp or EventBeginTransaction() 

Note: This occurs only once, before calling FireEventSp or FireApplicationEvent(). It uses a 
separate transaction to clear a storage area to record the row pointers of the event state 
rows to be created and returns a transaction identifier to be passed to FireEventSp or 
FireApplicationEvent(). 

2 

3 

Pass that transaction identifier to FireEventSp or FireApplicationEvent() 

Note: This, in a separate transaction, records the row pointer of the new event state row. 

Call EventEndTransactionSp or EventEndTransaction() and pass the transaction identifier 
and whether a commit or rollback will be/was performed 

Note: Perform this step upon rolling back or committing the transaction (it does not matter 
whether before or after). This uses a separate transaction to set the Rolled Back setting on 
the stored event state rows (if rolling back), and clears the storage area. 

If this is neglected, the system administrator cannot determine why database actions that appear to have 
finished successfully (according to the status forms) are not reflected in the database. 

In asynchronous situations, and when a failure is detected by the event system, the Rolled Back setting 
for the failing transactional event is set by the event system.

The Infor Framework Event Service 

The Infor Framework Event Service is an independent process that can run on any utility server. Each 
instance of the Infor Framework Event Service monitors the event queue for any number of configurations 
defined locally. 

For load-balancing purposes, this service safely acquires work from the database queue of each 
configuration's application, so that multiple event services can run on one or multiple servers. The event 
service monitors the event queue for: 

•  New events that have been queued by means of asynchronous generation methods or 

suspending framework events, as found in the Queued Event table. 

•  New event handlers that have been queued by means of the firing of asynchronous event 

handlers, as found in the Queued Event Handler table. 

Setting up the service 

To set up the Infor Framework Event Service to monitor specified configurations, you must specify each 
configuration individually on the Event Service tab of the Service Configuration Manager utility. Note: If 
you do not do this setup, any event handler you create that requires the event service will not run. 

For more information, including the procedure, see CloudSuite online help for the Service 
Configuration Manager utility. 

If you want to use the IDO Runtime Development Server (IDORuntimeHost.exe) in your development 
work, you must also temporarily remove the dependency that the event service has on the IDO Runtime 
service.  

For more information, including the procedure to do this, see the online help for WinStudio 
developers: From the Help menu, select Customizing Forms; then, when the help window 
opens, select the topic "Running IDO Runtime Development Server." 

Processing order  

The Infor Framework Event Service processes any queued events in "first in, first out" (FIFO) order. 
Because the event service could receive a request to run something while it is executing a prior request, 
all requests are queued for execution in the order requested. If the new request happens to be the only 
one in the queue and the event service is not busy, the request is executed at the next polling. 

When the event queue is empty, and immediately after processing each queued event or event handler, 
the event service checks the Event Handler State and Event Trigger tables for any items whose Retest At 
time setting has arrived or passed. 

The event service then checks the Event Handler State table for any items for which the timeout has 
expired, that is, the Times Out At time setting has arrived or passed. The event service processes these 
in order (that is, oldest first), using the Retest At time or Times Out At time to determine the order. 

After this, the event service checks the event queue again for any incoming items. 

52   Lesson 2: Understanding how the system works 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Administrative details 

To eliminate the possibility of deadlocks when manipulating event data, a second database connection is 
used for event data modifications, that is, separate from the one used by the event action execution 
methods. Also, any event data modifications that do not need to be available to other processes—that is, 
any tier of any interactive user session or other event service instances—are made in memory and written 
only when required. 

To avoid overtaxing the utility server, the Framework Event Service administrator has control over the 
minimum interval for these attributes: 

•  RetestInterval parameter on Wait event actions 
• 
Interval parameter on Sleep event actions 
•  RetestInterval attributes on event triggers 

When any of these attributes is referenced, if the designer-specified value is greater than zero but lower 
than the minimum interval for the Infor Framework Event Service, the minimum value is used. 

The Infor Framework Event Service logs various levels of messages to the Infor Framework Log Monitor. 

Troubleshooting 

The Infor Framework Event Service must be running for events and event handlers to execute. If you 
want to explore why your events are not executing as you expect, try these troubleshooting actions: 

•  First, investigate is whether the Infor Framework Event Service has started and is running.  
•  Next, verify is that your configuration has been added to the event service so that you can use the 

event service. This is done in the Event Service Configuration Manager.  

Note: Your configuration is the CloudSuite instance that you select from the drop-down list when 
you log in to the application.  

Demo: Utility services and verifying the event service setup

Access As identifier 

When first learning about the Application Event System, it is important to understand the Access As 
identifier and how and why the system uses it.  

The Access As identifier: 

• 
Indicates who (what organization) created and "owns" an event or related event system object. 
•  Prevents unauthorized developers from modifying or deleting event system objects that they do 

not own. 

On many forms in the Application Event System, the Access As field indicates who has ownership of the 
pertinent object metadata. You cannot modify or delete metadata for event system objects that you do not 
own. However, you can use without changing them.  

For instance, you will often use events created by Infor and shipped with the Infor CloudSuite Application 
Event System, called framework or core events. You can attach your own event actions and handlers to 
these events—you just cannot directly modify or delete the event system objects.  

Classifications for Access As 

Generally, the Access As identifier falls into one of three classifications: 

•  Core – Indicates that the object is one that Infor created and owns. These event system objects 

are used for the framework forms and operations.  

•  Any other name – Indicates that the object was created by and belongs to an application created 

either by Infor or by one of Infor’s business partners or other authorized vendors. 
•  Blank – Indicates that the object was created by and belongs to the end customer. 

Which of the three classifications do you think will apply to you as you create events and 
event system objects during this course? Please consider your response before reading the 
answer below.

Access As form 

The Access As form is a display-only form and is not directly used to create or customize application 
events and handlers. The form displays the current Access As setting for your user, which indicates which 
event system elements you are authorized to modify and/or delete. Note: In most cases, this field should 
be left blank.  

If you are an Infor business partner or authorized vendor developer and you need to 
change the Access As identifier, contact your Infor Consulting Services (ICS) 
representative for help.

Events  

An event, as we learned earlier, is defined as a uniquely named situation that can be triggered by: 

•  An action performed by somebody working in the system. 
•  A condition that occurs while the system is running. 
•  A certain value in a database record that exceeds a set threshold level.  
•  Another event’s handler. 
•  Other, similar occurrences. 

Event types 

Events can be classified as one of three general types shown in the following diagram: 

Customer-
defined events

Application-
specific events

Framework 
events

Created by a 
developer in an 
end-customer 
organization.

Tagged with a blank 
Access As identifier, 
which indicates that 
they were created 
by and belong to 
the customer.

Created by Infor, its 
business partners, 
and authorized 
vendors. 

Tagged with an 
Access As identifier 
that indicates what 
application or 
organization they 
belong to.

Created by Infor as 
part of the system.

Tagged with an 
Access As identifier 
of “core.” 

IDO events

Session events

Event classifications 

Framework events generally fall into one of two categories: 

• 

IDO events: These framework events are business process-related and are generated when 
certain IDOs are invoked. You can identify these events easily by their names, which begin with 
the letters “Ido.” 

•  Session events: These framework events are generated when certain session activities take 
place. These include SessionOnLogin, SessionOnLogout, and SessionOnVarChanged. Note: 
These events are always synchronous and transactional. Some can be optionally suspended to 
await user responses. 

A list of all framework events can be found in Appendix A.

Events form 

The Events form is used to name events. Once named here, events are available on other forms as well, 
including the Event Triggers and Event Handlers forms. 

An event named on the Events form and is just that: a name. Until you define a way for it to be triggered 
or initiated, the event remains just a name. This can be done using either the Event Triggers form, the 
Event Handlers form, or both.  

Note: An event can be named on other forms (Event Triggers or Event Handlers form), but be aware 
that an event name defined on another form will not display on the Events form.

Event triggers  

An event trigger is defined as a condition that causes a particular event to fire, more or less independent 
of anything that might be happening in the user interface. The event trigger carries a set of event trigger 
parameters for use when the event fires. 

You can set an event trigger to fire the event only once, or you can set it to retest for its condition (and fire 
again) at certain time intervals after: 

•  The trigger last successfully fired the event. 
•  The trigger last tested unsuccessfully for its condition. 

In both cases, you have control over the interval to wait, both for the successful firing of the trigger and for 
the unsuccessful test for the trigger conditions (using separate settings). Testing and retesting is 
accomplished by means of polling; this is not a true interruptive trigger. 

An event trigger carries with it the user name and configuration in effect at the time it was defined. This 
data is passed on to the event state when the trigger fires the event. 

You cannot define a trigger for a framework event. 

Event Triggers form 

The Event Triggers form is used to define event triggers. Use this form to determine the condition that 
will fire the event, set the parameters to be passed to the event when it fires, and specify retest intervals. 

Here is how to create an event trigger with the Event Triggers form: 

If you are 
going to … 

Create an 
event trigger 

Then … 

•  Open the Event Triggers form.  
•  Click Filter in Place. 
•  Click Create a new object in the current collection.  
•  Select an event from the Event Name drop-down list. 
•  Type the conditions at which the event is to fire.  

•  Type the name and values for any event parameters for which you need to 

pass values to the event handlers when the event fires.  

•  Click Save. 

For more information on other form options, please see the online help for each field. 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

63 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Conditions 

Each event trigger must contain a condition consisting of one of these: 

•  A Boolean expression 
•  Two non-Boolean expressions separated by a comparison operator 

Let’s look at three examples of how to write condition statements.  

Example 1  Fire event every seven days 

This example causes the event to fire when seven days have elapsed since the current result of the 
database function dbo.LastEntryDate(): 

DATEDIFF(day, DBFUNCTION("LastEntryDate"), CURDATETIME()) > 7 

Example 2  Fire event for customer orders over $10,000 

This example causes the event to fire when the balance on a certain customer's order is greater than 
$10,000: 

DBFUNCTION("OrderBalance", GC(BigCustNum)) > 10000 

Example 3  Fire event at the beginning of each month 

This example causes the event to fire on the first day of each month: 

DATEPART(day, CURDATETIME()) = 1 

End of examples  

The event trigger conditions you create should include an element that changes, which will generally be 
either: 

•  A time operation 
•  A database calculation 

Or 

•  Both a time operation and a database calculation 

This is because time and the database are the only known factors that can undergo change from external 
stimuli. Time changes from the forward movement of time; database calculations change because of the 
actions of application users. 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

64   Lesson 3: Using basic system elements 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Retesting triggers 

When the event trigger's Retest At Date setting becomes older than the current system clock time, the 
event trigger is available to be processed by the event service. This happens when the event service is 
free from processing any waiting queued events and handlers and any already-waiting triggers that need 
to be tested or retested. 

To process the event trigger, the system parses the condition and evaluates it as either true or false. 

If the condition evaluates to True, then...

If the condition evaluates to False, then...

The event fires.

The event does not fire.

The Retest At Date is set to:           

Current time + Trigger Reset Interval.

The Retest At Date is set to:           

Current time + Condition Retest 
Interval.

The Active indicator is cleared and 
the trigger will not be retested, if the 
Trigger Reset Interval = 0.    

The Active indicator is cleared and 
the trigger will not be retested, if the 
Condition Retest Interval = 0.

_________________________________________________________________________

Event handlers and event actions 

Event handlers and event actions work closely together and we will cover them both in this lesson.  

Event handlers 

An event handler defines what actions are to be taken upon the firing of a particular event. Each event 
handler is comprised of one or more event actions and, optionally, an initial state. 

Each event can have multiple event handlers that execute when the event fires. In such cases, the 
handler sequence number and other factors determine the order in which event handlers are processed. 

Event actions 

An event action is a unit of work to be performed during the execution of an event handler. A single event 
handler can have multiple event actions, but each action is assigned to a single event handler. Depending 
on its action type, an event action can do such things as: 

•  Evaluate and compare expressions, using the results to select which event action of its event 

handler to perform next 
•  Affect the event's visual state 
•  Complete the event handler 
•  Set event variables 
•  Call methods or web services 
•  Perform other predefined tasks 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

_________________________________________________________________________ 

Event Handlers form  

The Event Handlers form is used to display and define event handlers which determine the work to be 
done when an event fires. Each event handler is uniquely defined in the system by the combination of an 
event name and a handler sequence number. Event handlers also must have one or more associated 
event actions.  

Here is how to create an event handler:

Other options for event handlers 

You can restrict an event handler so that it executes only in relation to a specific set of conditions. For 
example, a particular event handler might be defined to execute only when the associated event is 
triggered by an action on a particular form or when a particular IDO is involved. 

You can set an event handler to execute synchronously or asynchronously, or as part of a transactional 
event or set of event handlers.  

You can control the order in which the event handler executes by using the Keep With and Chronology 
fields, or the Event Handler Sequence form.

Now that you have been introduced to some of the components of the Application Event System, the next

Handlers, and Event Actions), you will also see a new form called the Edit Action Parameters form. The

Create a simple handler for a framework event: IdoPostItemInsert  

In the exercise that follows, you do not need to create an event, because Infor provides a framework 
event named IdoPostItemInsert that you can use. All you need to do is create an event handler for that 
event and assign an action to it that generates and sends the message to the credit manager. 

We could use the IdoOnItemInsert framework event instead of the IdoPostItemInsert event. The 
advantage of using the IdoPostItemInsert event is that, if you allow the Customers form to auto-assign 
the customer number (instead of specifying the customer ID number yourself), the system waits until the 
ID number has been assigned before filling in the "CustNum" data in the message. If we use the 
IdoOnItemInsert event, the system does not wait, which means that, if you auto-assign the customer ID 
number, the resulting message has "TBD" in place of the actual customer number.

Event action parameters  

Event action parameters are settings that further define certain types of event actions. There are two 
ways to specify event action parameters:  

•  Type parameters directly into the Event Actions form. 
•  Use the Event Action Parameters form to define the parameters. 

You can specify parameters in any order for an action. To list multiple parameters, specify them one after 
another, specifying either a space or nothing between them.  

When you use the Event Action Parameters form, the parameters are returned to the 
Event Actions form properly formatted and free of syntax errors. 

Syntax for parameters 

The syntax for each event action parameter is as follows: FUNCTION(value). 

The value enclosed in parentheses can consist of: 

•  A constant number 
•  A literal string enclosed in quotation marks 
•  A Boolean value: TRUE or FALSE 
•  An event function call 

Note: These can be nested.  

•  An expression consisting of a number of these elements combined using operators 

Although it is not a requirement that function names be specified using all uppercase letters, we 
recommend the practice, as it leads to greater ease of recognition and readability. 

Example   Simple event parameters 

In exercise 3.2, you created several simple event parameters. The parameters are noted in the following 
table:  

Event parameters 

Value in the parentheses 

CATEGORY("Change Notification") 

Literal string 

TO("userID") 

Constant  

SUBJECT("New Customer!")  

Literal string 

BODY("We have a new customer!")  

Literal string 

SAVEMESSAGE(TRUE) 

Boolean value 

End of example  

72   Lesson 3: Using basic system elements 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
For a list of all acceptable event action parameters, including their meaning and examples, 
please see Appendix A. For complete expression grammar for constructing event action 
parameters, please see Appendix B.  

Function types 

Functions can be any of three basic types: 

•  Parameter functions 
•  Value functions 
•  Word functions 

Let’s look further at each type.  

A parameter function is one where the parentheses wrap a parameter to the event action. These 
functions must always appear at the root level and can never be nested inside any other type of function.  

Here are some typical parameter functions: 

•  SETVARVALUES 
•  METHOD 
• 
INTERVAL 
•  EVENTNAME 

Parameter functions are identified in this documentation generically as PARAMS(…). 

A value function is one that calls an event value. These types of functions can never appear at the root 
level but must be nested within another function construct, either a parameter or another function. 

Here are some typical value functions: 

•  SUBSTITUTE 
•  DATE 
•  ABS 
•  CEILING 

Value functions are identified in this documentation generically as FUNCTION(…). 

A word function is verbatim words used inside certain event function calls. Note that some of these can 
also be used as event function calls, but word functions always appear within an event function call.  

Here are some typical word functions: 

•  AS 
•  STRING 
•  NUMBER 
•  DAY 
•  DATE 

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

73 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Value functions are identified in this documentation generically as …WORD… 

Nesting function calls 

When building event parameters, you can also use the parentheses following the function to wrap 
expressions that signify operations to be performed on the results of the expression.  

When defining event action parameters, keep these nesting rules in mind: 

Function type 

Nesting rules 

PARAMS(…) 

Cannot be nested 

FUNCTION(…)  Must be nested within either: 

•  PARAMS(…) functions 

•  FUNCTION(…) functions 

…WORD… 

Must be nested ONLY within: 

•  FUNCTION(…) functions 

This is an example of an event action parameter that uses nested functions: 

PARAMS(…FUNCTION1(…FUNCTION2(…WORD1…)…WORD2…)…) 

Passing parameters from actions 

Parameter lists to methods, scripts, Web services, and generated events are always enclosed in a 
PARAMS(…) function and delimited by commas.  

Each parameter is specified as shown in the following table: 

Syntax 

Direction 

Meaning 

V(var) 

Input 

Pass in the value of the variable var 

expression 

Input 

Pass in the value obtained by evaluating the expression 

RV(var) 

Input and 
output 

Pass in the value of the variable var, and place the output value into the 
same variable

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission.

Event Action Parameters forms 

Event Action Parameters forms are used to define the event action parameters for each action type. 

There is a set of Event Action Parameters forms, not a single form. Each event action 
type has its own corresponding form for defining parameters.  

To open the Event Action Parameters form, click the Edit Parameters button found on 
the Event Actions form.  

To make it easier to create event action parameters that are properly defined and formatted, each event 
action type has a corresponding Event Action Parameter form. These forms allow you to build 
appropriate sets of parameters for each action type by providing options that pertain to that action type. 

Very few options on any given form are considered required. As a rule, only those fields that you populate 
return parameter values to the Event Actions form. Where strings require quotation marks, the system 
inserts them automatically. Where expressions are nested inside other expressions or parameter 
statements, the system automatically places parentheses where they are required. 

The result is that, by using the Event Action Parameter forms, you can eliminate most, or more likely all, 
syntax errors in your parameter statements.

Event variables and initial states 

If an event handler has variables associated with it, those variables can be assigned an initial state. An 
initial state is a set of values assigned when the event handler starts to execute.  

Each initial state consists of: 

•  A name that identifies it in the system. 
•  Any number of event variables with the initial values they are to have when the event handler 

starts to execute. 

Example   Initial states 

For example, you might want to use variables with these initial values with your event handlers: 

Variable 

Initial value 

Comments 

Increment 

ItemTypes

None 

None 

TimesRemaining 

GC(MaxTimes) 

In this case, the value is determined by the value of the 
MaxTimes global constant. 

End of example  

Event Variable Groups form 

The Event Variable Groups form is used to display and define initial states for sets of event variables. 
The initial states are the values to be passed to the event handler when it starts to run. 

You can use an initial state you have already created with any other event handler. To use an existing 
initial state, select it in the Initial State field on the Event Handlers form.

Event global constants 

An event global constant is defined as a named static value that event expressions can reference during 
processing of the associated event handlers. In simple terms, an event global constant is a placeholder 
for a stored value. The advantage of using event global constants is that a value is not hard-coded in the 
handler. This means you can easily change the value of the constant in one place without having to 
revise all the event handlers that reference that value.  

Event Global Constants form 

Event global constants are defined using the Event Global Constants form. There are only two editable 
fields on this form: 

•  Name – the name you choose for the global constant 
•  Value – the value or values you want to assign to this global constant 

The system references a global constant using a function mechanism that allows dynamic evaluation at 
each reference. 

Now we will look at two examples for using an event global constant.  

Example 1  Event global constant for a job title 

Let’s say you have one individual in your organization, Pat, who holds the title of credit manager. In this 
role, Pat is authorized to review and set customer credit limits. You can create an event global constant 
named CreditManager to refer to Pat and then use it in a variety of ways. You can set up event handlers 
to:  

•  Email the CreditManager when an order exceeds a customer's credit limit.  
•  Notify the CreditManager when a customer has been placed on credit hold for some reason.  
•  Prompt the CreditManager for a response to a request for an increase to a customer's credit limit.  

Later, then, if Pat no longer has the credit manager role, all you have to do is replace Pat’s name with the 
name of the individual who is in the credit manager role, and all the CreditManager actions will reference 
the new individual. 

Example 2  Event global constant for a yes/no prompt 

Your manager wants you to set up a series of events to ask various people to vote yes or no on every 
new proposal that comes in. You decide event global constants will be useful for defining a set of choices 
to offer the recipients of a prompt message for each new proposal.  

You create a new global constant with the name PromptChoicesYesNo and define it with the value 
1,sYes,0,sNo. You then reference this constant for all prompt event action, using the expression: 
CHOICES(GC(PromptChoicesYesNo)). 

End of examples

Setting up custom events and handlers 

We have already learned about the predefined events that Infor has built in to the Application Event 
System. There are predefined event handlers as well that are available for your immediate use. In 
addition, if you have an add-on product distributed by one of Infor’s business partners, they might have 
also added their own events and handlers that you can use. 

If you have special requirements, you can design and create your own custom events and handlers to 
automate tasks for your needs. This lesson provides information about the procedures for creating and 
using custom events and handlers. 

To create and use your own custom application events, there are several important steps and 
considerations to keep in mind. You must: 

•  Design and define your custom event.  
•  Carefully plan and set up the order in which event handlers and actions execute.  
•  Periodically discard the metadata cache, to ensure that you are working with the most current 

version of the event metadata.  

We will look at each in further detail.  

v10 Using the Application Event System Training Workbook (10.18) 

85 

© 2018 Infor Education. All rights reserved. No part of this Training Workbook may be reproduced or transmitted in any form, without written permission. 

 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Designing a custom event 

When you create a custom event, you must also define what will fire the event. In the current WinStudio 
system, there are four ways to generate a custom event: 

•  Create an event trigger using the Event Trigger form.  
•  Use an action of type Generate Event in another event handler. 
•  Use a form (WinStudio) event handler with a Response Type of Generate Application Event. (Do 
not confuse the form, or WinStudio events with the Application Event System handlers described 
in this guide.) 

•  Use the WinStudio API to write a custom script that generates an event.  

The easiest and most common way to generate a custom event is to create a trigger using 
the Event Trigger form. 

The following is a high-level overview of a typical process for creating custom application events: 

Step 

How to create a custom application event 

1 

Name the event 

•  If you want to name the event before defining how it should be triggered and/or handled, 

use the Events form. 

•  Names will appear on the Name drop-down lists on the Event Triggers and Event 

Handlers forms. You can name events named on those forms, however, the name will 
not display on the Events form.  

2 

3 

4 

5 

6 

Define the triggers (optional) 

To define one or more triggers that will fire the event, use the Event Triggers form. 

Define the event handlers 
•  To define one or more event handlers that will execute when the event fires, use the 

Event Handlers form. 

•  Each event can have multiple handlers that execute when the event fires. The order in 

which multiple handlers execute is controlled by several factors.  

Define the event actions 

To define one or more event actions for each event handler, use the Event Actions form. 

Define an initial state (optional) 

If required to name and define an initial state for the event handler to use, use the Event 
Variable Groups form. 

Define global constants (optional) 

If required to name and define any global constants for the event handler to use, use the 
Event Global Constants form.

live system.
